Based on the position, it looks like white’s queen on b6 is attacking both of black’s rooks along the key diagonal (b6–c7–d8). (Note that the rook on c7 is defended by the black king on b8 but the rook on d8 is hanging.) One good idea is to reposition the rook on d8 so that the two rooks end up helping each other. For example, by playing

  d8c8

the rook on d8 moves along the 8th rank to c8, thereby joining the rook already on c7 on the same file. Now if white tries to grab one of them (say by capturing on c7), black will be able to recapture (for instance, Rxc7) and win the white queen for a rook—a trade white will not allow.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

The black king on b8 is currently in check from white’s queen on b6 along the b‐file (with b7 empty between them). In order to get out of check, black must either move the king or block the check. (There’s no possibility to capture the queen.)

If Black moves the king, the only safe option is Kb8→a8. However, that leaves the rook on c7 undefended along the b6–c7 diagonal, so White could capture on c7 next, winning a rook for a queen.

A better solution is to block the check. The only square between b6 and b8 is b7. The rook sitting on c7 can move horizontally one square from c7 to b7, interposing the check. (Note that if White subsequently takes the rook on b7 with Qxb7, Black can recapture with the king, and then Black will have exchanged a rook for White’s queen—which is clearly not in White’s interest.)

Therefore, I will play:

make_move c7b7

That move puts the white king (on c2) in check along the c‐file while keeping our queen safely out of immediate reach (it won’t be adjacent to the white king, nor is it on a square accessible to the white bishop on a2).

• Our queen on d6 can legally slide diagonally to c5.
• On c5 the queen gives a direct check along the c‐file down to white’s king on c2.
• None of white’s pieces (the pawn on a5, pawn on g5, or bishop on a2) can immediately capture our queen on c5.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Based on the current position, I believe the best plan is to bring my king closer to support the pawn before advancing it. If I pushed the pawn from b4 to b3 immediately, it would sit unprotected and the white king on c2 could capture it on b3 (since b3 would be adjacent to white king).

Instead, I want to move my king from c5 to c4. With the king on c4, it will protect the crucial square b3. Then, once I advance the pawn by playing b4b3, the white king will not be able to capture it because b3 will be guarded by my king (remember that kings cannot move into squares adjacent to the opposing king). This is the classic technique of escorting a passed pawn in a king-and-pawn versus king endgame.

So, my move is:

make_move c5c4

• Black’s material is very limited (only a king plus six pawns), so counterplay must come entirely from pawn advances and king safety.
• The pawn on g7 is still on its original square. Pushing it to g6 not only gives the king additional “luft” (an escape square), but also begins to mobilize Black’s kingside pawn majority.
• Other pawn moves (like advancing b5, c5, or f5) are likely less urgent, and moving the king without easing the cramped nature of Black’s position might allow White more energetic checks.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

After reviewing the board, one promising option is to improve king safety. In the current position the black pieces are limited – with king on g8 and three pawns (on a6, g4, and a2) – and the g4 pawn is already under the white queen’s attack along the fourth rank. Moving a pawn (like g4→g3) would only delay its capture, while the a6 and a2 pawns are essentially blocked. 

The best resource appears to be repositioning the king. From g8, the legal king moves are to f8, f7, or g7. Notably, moving to g7 would align the king vertically with the white queen if it captures on g4 (since the queen on h4 controls the h–file and moving horizontally from there can bring it to an aligned file with the king on g7). In contrast, shifting the king to f8 avoids such immediate alignment, keeping the king slightly tucked away on the back rank.

Therefore, I will move my king from g8 to f8.

Based on the diagram’s coordinates (with the top row being rank 8 and the bottom row rank 1), here is a summary of the position:

• Black has only a king on c7 and a pawn on a2.
• White has two pawns (on a5 and b5), a queen on f6, a knight on c2, and the king on g4.

Looking at Black’s resources, the pawn on a2 can move forward one square (to a1) and promote—but note that white’s knight on c2 has a legal jump to a1 (since from c2 the knight can go to a1), immediately capturing any promoted piece. That makes a pawn move less attractive.

Thus, Black’s only real hope is to safely reposition the king. From c7 the king’s legal moves (ignoring any move that would step into an attacked square) are:
  • c7–b8
  • c7–c8
  • c7–b7
  • c7–d7

A quick look at the threats shows that moving toward the “back rank” is a reasonable defensive choice. For example, a move to b8 places the king farther from the action and avoids the white queen’s – which on f6 controls horizontally (rank 6) plus the two main diagonals that include d8 and a1. Also, none of white’s pieces (the queen, knight, or pawns) can immediately target b8.

Therefore, a good candidate move for Black is to shift the king from c7 to b8. In UCI notation that move is written as:

make_move c7b8

I’ll now play that move.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

I'll play the move: make_move e4f2

Here's my reasoning:

On the board, my knight currently sits on e4 and by moving it to f2 you capture White’s pawn on f2. More importantly, sitting on f2 the knight immediately threatens to jump to d1 (a legal knight move from f2) on the next move, which would attack White’s queen on d1. This forces White to react. In many positions, such a move creates serious tactical headaches for the opponent, as it either wins material or forces awkward king moves.

So I choose e4f2.

In this position no black piece can capture the white queen on c3, and none can interpose on the c‑file (the dark‑squared bishop on f8 is blocked by your pawn on e7, and your other pieces cannot reach one of the blocking squares on c4–c7). The only available way to respond is to move your king.
